<div id="review_2181795487" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2181795487" />
    <a title="Tracey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37756416-tracey"><img alt="Tracey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589946289p2/37756416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2181795487?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tracey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tracey" href="/user/show/37756416-tracey">Tracey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780449912461" href="/book/show/672947.The_Road_Back">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2181795487" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6684749262840186277">This is the sequel to All Quiet on the Western Front. If you have read that I recommend that you be sure to read this. The two go hand in hand. <br/><br/>The story begins in Flanders with men still fighting and dying and being maimed, whilst the talk of peace ripples along the front line with a mixture of hope and disbelief.<br/><br/>Those who 'survive' slowly make their way home only to discover that nothing is as they remember because they themselves have been forever changed by the horrors of war.<br/><br/>There is a real</span>
  <span id="freeText6684749262840186277" style="display:none">This is the sequel to All Quiet on the Western Front. If you have read that I recommend that you be sure to read this. The two go hand in hand. <br /><br />The story begins in Flanders with men still fighting and dying and being maimed, whilst the talk of peace ripples along the front line with a mixture of hope and disbelief.<br /><br />Those who 'survive' slowly make their way home only to discover that nothing is as they remember because they themselves have been forever changed by the horrors of war.<br /><br />There is a real insight into the both the PTSD and the state of Germany as it collapses in the aftermath. I learned a lot of this time period from one who was there.<br /><br />The author's writing at times is sharp and jarring, and at others flows like pure water; as if Remarque himself was slipping between the hallucinatory world of the war and what it had done to him, and the romantic, idealistic educated young man he once was, as he wrote of these young comrades home from one hell into another kind of hell.<br /><br />There were many parts of this book where I just wanted to weep at the psychological damage done to these men and the walking wounded of a returned generation.<br /><br />Many scenes stand out in my mind but one in particular, a scene from later in the book: a march of returned soldiers who have come home only to find poverty, penury, and a world no longer valuing them or the sacrifice they made. At the front of the march are men with one arm missing carrying the banners. Then comes the blind with eyes shot out. Then those who have a little sight but their faces are damaged and many have deformed or no jaws/mouth, noses. Next comes those who had 1 leg amputated shuffling along on crutches, followed by those with both legs lost, pushed along in wicker wheeled chairs. And finally, men who are just a mere torso pushed along on trolleys like the type used to move carcasses of meat in an abattoir. <br /><br />'awful misery, without much hope, prisoners of the destiny that others made for them.'<br /><br />The journal I keep of quotes from books I read is full of wisdom and truth when writing from this book.<br /><br />" We were making war against ourselves without knowing it! Every shot that struck home, struck one of us."<br /><br />"The youth of the world rose up in every land, believing that it was fighting for freedom. And in every land, they were duped and misused."<br /><br />This is a book I would like a copy of to keep on my bookshelf and hope others will read. <br /><br /> 'all learning, all culture, all science is nothing but hideous mockery, so long as mankind makes war in the name of God and humanity with gas, iron, explosive and fire.' We need to remember and learn from the testimony of these fallen, or suffer it all over again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6684749262840186277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2086026449" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2086026449" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37929896-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1423941056p2/37929896.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2086026449?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/37929896-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37929896-josh?shelf=the-great-war">the-great-war</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2086026449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15989609346906822819"><b>"Yes, it is a hard thing to part, but to come back again, that is sometimes harder."</b><br/><br/>This sequel to <i>All Quiet on the Western Front</i> (AQ) is less famous, but I liked it better. While AQ focused on men in the trenches during WWI and the horror they faced, <i>The Road Back</i> tells of the same men as they return to their old homes.<br/><br/>Many of these guys went off to the war while they were still boys, full of piss and vinegar and patriotism. As they lived the horrors described in AQ for 3 or 4 years, everything</span>
  <span id="freeText15989609346906822819" style="display:none"><b>"Yes, it is a hard thing to part, but to come back again, that is sometimes harder."</b><br /><br />This sequel to <i>All Quiet on the Western Front</i> (AQ) is less famous, but I liked it better. While AQ focused on men in the trenches during WWI and the horror they faced, <i>The Road Back</i> tells of the same men as they return to their old homes.<br /><br />Many of these guys went off to the war while they were still boys, full of piss and vinegar and patriotism. As they lived the horrors described in AQ for 3 or 4 years, everything at home went on as if they hadn't left. Only how can one not change during the experience of war? And when one returns, how can they fit neatly back into the space in society they had left? It is like changing the shape of a missing puzzle piece and then trying to fit it back into the whole puzzle, which had also changed shape.<br /><br />And the pieces do not fit back into place. Spouses have found other lovers in their absence, formerly well-respected adults are now just men who were not there to guide the boys from childhood to manhood, etc.<br /><br />I really liked this book and it is as relevant today as it was when it was written. I am told that the third of this "trilogy" by Remarque <i>Three Comrades</i> is also very good so I will seek it out and read that as well. I have been impressed with Remarque's writing, but can't put my finger on why. It's so simplistic, but maybe that's why it's so easy to read. Perhaps I will seek out more of his writings after this trilogy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15989609346906822819" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer7511125225982209378">A sort of sequel to All Quiet on the Western Front, although only one minor character from that book makes an appearance. This is a group of young men from the same area, so some of the dead, including Paul Baumer, are referenced. These are still very young men, for the most part. At 19 or 20 they return to school to get their degrees so they can have some future. This covers the first year after the war and the book effectively conveys the frustration and melancholic mood of the these former so</span>
  <span id="freeText7511125225982209378" style="display:none">A sort of sequel to All Quiet on the Western Front, although only one minor character from that book makes an appearance. This is a group of young men from the same area, so some of the dead, including Paul Baumer, are referenced. These are still very young men, for the most part. At 19 or 20 they return to school to get their degrees so they can have some future. This covers the first year after the war and the book effectively conveys the frustration and melancholic mood of the these former soldiers. It is a very sincere book. The writing, especially much of the dialogue is stagy and stilted (much like Lew Ayres's acting in the movie version of All Quiet), but (just like the movie) is still moving. Without their uniforms, the relationships among these men is all turned upside down. A poor soldier who comes from a middle class background can now lord it over a working man's son who was his superior. The denser a man's mind, the quicker he recovers, while the more sensitive and intelligent suffer. Solid citizens who never left their town, complain about how the war could have been won. Socialism, a wife, the dream of a future home are all empty dreams that each man hopes will bring him back to something like normalcy. This book is told in the first person by a young man named Ernst, who finds what comfort he can from nature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7511125225982209378" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2791717778" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2791717778" />
    <a title="Ellie Midwood" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33539108-ellie-midwood"><img alt="Ellie Midwood" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473347829p2/33539108.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2791717778?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellie Midwood" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellie Midwood" href="/user/show/33539108-ellie-midwood">Ellie Midwood</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19436311-the-road-back">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33539108-ellie-midwood?shelf=wwi-fiction">wwi-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33539108-ellie-midwood?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33539108-ellie-midwood?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33539108-ellie-midwood?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2791717778" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11851463151655354334">An incredibly touching and tragic follow-up to the most remarkable anti-war novel “All Quiet on the Western Front,” “The Road Back” is a must-read for everyone who enjoys great literature and history combined. The plot follows Ernst and his comrades who, after the Great War is over, find themselves restless and abandoned by their own country and compatriots. The armistice has just been signed, yet for them, the road back to civilian life will be long and difficult, and not everyone will be able </span>
  <span id="freeText11851463151655354334" style="display:none">An incredibly touching and tragic follow-up to the most remarkable anti-war novel “All Quiet on the Western Front,” “The Road Back” is a must-read for everyone who enjoys great literature and history combined. The plot follows Ernst and his comrades who, after the Great War is over, find themselves restless and abandoned by their own country and compatriots. The armistice has just been signed, yet for them, the road back to civilian life will be long and difficult, and not everyone will be able to adapt. The signs of PTSD, turmoil surrounding them, political unrest, and the new life that they simply can’t comprehend any longer - all this is brilliantly delivered through Remarque’s beautiful prose and memorable characters. Certain scenes will move you to tears while some will cause rightful indignation as the first paramilitary units - future SS and Hitlerjugend squads - begin to rear their ugly heads. “My God, Willy… here we are alive still and only just out of it - how, in God’s name, is it possible there should already be such people to do that sort of thing?” (P. 310 paperback edition) Brutally honest and impossibly lyrical, this novel is a deserving sequel to an immortal classic, “All Quiet on the Western Front.” </span>
  <a data-text-id="11851463151655354334" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1724957090" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1724957090" />
    <a title="Michael J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34775132-michael-j"><img alt="Michael J." src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1724957090?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael J." href="/user/show/34775132-michael-j">Michael J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1724957090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16950954027009546048">I heartily recommend this novel. Here is why:<br/><br/>Khizr Kahn’s speech at the Democratic National Convention and Donald Trump’s response to it has focused attention on the concepts of “homeland,” “patriotism,” and “sacrifice.” In these contentious times thoughtful debate about them is otiose. Literature may help us more to understand the concepts. With a book in hand a reader can damp down the discourse on social media and quietly contemplate the writer’s story. Now, there have been some very good nov</span>
  <span id="freeText16950954027009546048" style="display:none">I heartily recommend this novel. Here is why:<br /><br />Khizr Kahn’s speech at the Democratic National Convention and Donald Trump’s response to it has focused attention on the concepts of “homeland,” “patriotism,” and “sacrifice.” In these contentious times thoughtful debate about them is otiose. Literature may help us more to understand the concepts. With a book in hand a reader can damp down the discourse on social media and quietly contemplate the writer’s story. Now, there have been some very good novels and short stories collections about America’s misadventures in Iraq (Fobbit by David Abrams, Redeployment by Phil Klay, and Flashes of War by Katy Schultz). These works, however, are too contemporary; they lack perspective. The French philosopher Philippe Aries in his book The Hour of Our Death writes that we need a millennium to understand fully some concepts and ideals. We need not go so far back as that to find works to help us define these three terms. I suggest, instead, we seek out works to read from the Fin de Siecle and the interstices between the wars. After all, the social, economic, and geopolitical issues we argue over today have roots in this period of history. A twentieth-century novel that deserves a fresh look is Erich Maria Remarque’s The Road Back.<br /><br />The Road Back is the sequel to Remarque’s first and most famous novel, All Quiet on the Western Front. Both books were written more than a decade after the end of World War I. By then the public’s attitude about the conflict had shifted. The war no longer was a “great crusade”; instead, it was a source of carnage and waste. This notion gained credence through the poetry of the soldiers who fought, the fiction of Ford Maddox Ford, Frederic Manning, and Siegfried Sassoon (he was a poet too) and the memoirs of Robert Graves, Edmund Blunden, and Vera Brittain. This notion certainly is clear in All Quiet on the Western Front. The novel was successful in part too, I think, because it tells the story of Great War from the perspective of the Axis side. Its protagonist, Paul Baumer, is an idealistic German soldier fighting for his country, not some savage slacked on the blood of the poor Belgiums.<br /><br />The Road Back, on the other hand, takes the story out of the trenches and transfers it to civilian life. It recounts the travails of a cadre of soldiers adjusting to life after the armistice. Ernrst Birkholz, the protagonist, suffers from PTSD (shell sock back then). He is haunted by the memory of a soldier he killed:<br /><br />The crash of the explosion tears the air, splinters twanging—a cry goes up, long-drawn, frantic with <br />Horror. …The Englishman is lying clear in the open field; his two legs are blown off at the knees, the blood is pouring out; the bands of his puttees, far unrolled, trail out behind him like loose ribbons; he is lying on his belly; with his arms he paddles; his mouth is wide open, shirking. He heaves himself around and sees me. Then he props himself on his arms and rears his trunk like a seal; he shrieks at me and bleeds, bleeds.<br /><br />The war intrudes into the quotidian activities of Ernest and his friends. At a party given by his uncle Karl “the famous member of our family [who] has a villa and was chief paymaster during the war,” Ernst embarrasses himself during dinner. The aroma of “crisp, grilled chops” revives memory of a meal he shared with his comrades. Just a few minutes after the main course is served he realizes that “There I sit, just as we did then in Flanders, absent-minded, both elbows on the table, the bone in my two hands, my fingers covered in grease, gnawing off the last scraps of the chop. But the others are eating cleanly with knife and with fork.” In a more severe incident, Ernst and his comrades meet up in a tavern with a noncommissioned officer whom they knew from the war. One of Ernst comrades savagely beats the man because he believes that the officer was responsible for the death of his very close friend. Alienation and violence pervade their lives. As Ernst observes later on about life in peacetime:<br /><br />We are like those abandoned fields full of shell holes in France, no less peaceful than other ploughed lands about them, but in them are lying still the buried explosives, and until these shall have been dug out and cleared away, to plough will be a danger both to plougher and ploughed.<br /><br />The Road Back is a story of soldiers gamely trying to assimilate into a society ambivalent towards them. Since the novel does not describe overmuch the carnage the men lived through, the episodes it does recount are all the more powerful. A reader needs not possess detailed knowledge of Weimar Germany to enjoy the book. (The novel itself, for instance, makes only passing reference to the communist revolt of 1918 led by Rosa Luxemburg.) A reader’s critical distance from the events depicted in the novel—first published in 1931—contributes far more to the appreciation of the book. Based on what we know occurred in the decade following the publication of The Road Back, the words “homeland” “patriotism,” and “sacrifice” take on for us a chilling context. We are impelled to draw on these feeling when we hear political leaders and pundits speak these words today.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16950954027009546048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Without glamorising the war or lauding the heroism of those at the front-line the novel is highly successful in highlighting the dissension between ex-servicemen and civilian, both of whom live in equally incomprehensible worlds to the other. <br/><br/>Friendship, nature, life, death, murder, revolution thrown in to the </span>
  <span id="freeText12758708626519521171" style="display:none">The Way Back doesn't grab you by the shoulders and rattle you like its prequel All Quiet but it does a great job in bringing out the disenfranchisement of the War's demobilised survivors. Without glamorising the war or lauding the heroism of those at the front-line the novel is highly successful in highlighting the dissension between ex-servicemen and civilian, both of whom live in equally incomprehensible worlds to the other. <br /><br />Friendship, nature, life, death, murder, revolution thrown in to the mix for good measure. <div id="review_3111207015" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3111207015" />
    <a title="P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35171790-p"><img alt="P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444223928p2/35171790.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3111207015?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="P" href="/user/show/35171790-p">P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3111207015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13649473637782467372">This story about the bleakness faced by German soldiers when they returned battle-weary from the front at the end of WWI is well-told, but somewhat depressing at the same time. I'm a fan of Remarque's, having read and enjoyed - or maybe appreciated is a better word - both All's Quiet on the Western Front as well as his Arch of Triumph.<br/>Of the three, I liked this one the least, in a large part because of the translation, which had a heavy 'English' aspect to it. For example, the translator's inter</span>
  <span id="freeText13649473637782467372" style="display:none">This story about the bleakness faced by German soldiers when they returned battle-weary from the front at the end of WWI is well-told, but somewhat depressing at the same time. I'm a fan of Remarque's, having read and enjoyed - or maybe appreciated is a better word - both All's Quiet on the Western Front as well as his Arch of Triumph.<br />Of the three, I liked this one the least, in a large part because of the translation, which had a heavy 'English' aspect to it. For example, the translator's interpretation of German epithets included the insertion of way too many uses of the words 'bloody', and 'old man', and 'old boy', and even at one point when money was the subject, having the character say "I'll give you five 'pounds'," instead of using the more natural, and accurate, German currency, marks.<br /><br />But Remarque himself was a brilliant writer, who tackled the most difficult of times with trenchant insight, as only a veteran of war and it's aftermath could do. So while the topic itself was disconcerting, I'm glad I read this book. We all know the epigram: "War is Hell." Well, coming home can be hell, too</span>
  <a data-text-id="13649473637782467372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2504596623" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2504596623" />
    <a title="Sam Reaves" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8537441-sam-reaves"><img alt="Sam Reaves" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442459348p2/8537441.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2504596623?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Reaves" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Reaves" href="/user/show/8537441-sam-reaves">Sam Reaves</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2504596623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16511207316931242275">Outside Germany, everyone knows Remarque's All Quiet on the Western Front, but few people are aware of his other novels, which is a pity, because some of them are terrific, particularly those set in the turbulent Germany of the twenties. Remarque continued to write until his death in 1970, his novels reflecting an active life that saw him leave Germany in the thirties and live in exile, eventually settling in New York and becoming a U.S. citizen. <br/>The Road Back is a sequel to All Quiet, depicting</span>
  <span id="freeText16511207316931242275" style="display:none">Outside Germany, everyone knows Remarque's All Quiet on the Western Front, but few people are aware of his other novels, which is a pity, because some of them are terrific, particularly those set in the turbulent Germany of the twenties. Remarque continued to write until his death in 1970, his novels reflecting an active life that saw him leave Germany in the thirties and live in exile, eventually settling in New York and becoming a U.S. citizen. <br />The Road Back is a sequel to All Quiet, depicting the return of the surviving characters from that book to their home town in a defeated and chaotic Germany. While the political turmoil that would lead to the rise of the Nazis forms the background, the novel is mainly concerned with the devastating effect of war on young lives. As they struggle to resume disrupted careers and find a place in a society that is blind and deaf to their sacrifices, the trench war survivors become increasingly embittered as others prosper. Despair, suicide and murder lie in wait. <br />The trauma, disillusionment and alienation of combat veterans barely out of their teens are sadly still familiar to us a hundred years later. They didn't call it PTSD in Germany in 1919, but as Remarque shows, they had it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16511207316931242275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2553585283" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2553585283" />
    <a title="Jan vanTilburg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40019240-jan-vantilburg"><img alt="Jan vanTilburg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514254658p2/40019240.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2553585283?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan vanTilburg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan vanTilburg" href="/user/show/40019240-jan-vantilburg">Jan vanTilburg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/42196744-der-weg-zur-ck">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40019240-jan-vantilburg?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40019240-jan-vantilburg?shelf=tormented-souls">tormented-souls</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2553585283" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11021703832411382228">Very powerful. And...not a cheerfull experience. <br/>But... with a hopeful end. It took some time but Remarque seemed to have found some peace at last. But the Road Back was not an easy one:<br/>Gloom and doom for the comerades. But that is clearly what Remarque wanted to convey and he does it masterfully. The message comes over loud and clear: War is horrible. It destroys lives, even when one survives.<br/><br/>Starts where “All quiet on the Western Front” ends. The first chapter describes the end of the war and </span>
  <span id="freeText11021703832411382228" style="display:none">Very powerful. And...not a cheerfull experience. <br />But... with a hopeful end. It took some time but Remarque seemed to have found some peace at last. But the Road Back was not an easy one:<br />Gloom and doom for the comerades. But that is clearly what Remarque wanted to convey and he does it masterfully. The message comes over loud and clear: War is horrible. It destroys lives, even when one survives.<br /><br />Starts where “All quiet on the Western Front” ends. The first chapter describes the end of the war and how unreal it is to just walk away from the front.<br />They keep on looking back. Now they have to say goodbye to all their fallen comrades.<br />It’s heartbreaking. They wonder - “is it the lost years which will stay there, is it the comrades who are lying there, is it all the misery that is covered by this dirt? -, there is such sorrow in their bones, that they could cry.”<br /><br />And then they go....<br />The road back is one big goodbye from each other. From the ones they lost. Behind them marches the army of the dead.... Gone, but not forgotten. They feel lost. All they know is the army and their comrades, their friends. It’s all they know.<br />And then they arrive home...<br /><br />Being home is not easy. They only feel “at home” when they are with their fellow soldiers.<br />Nobody understands what they went thru.<br />One of them realized that they need healing. He himself goes back in the army. It’s all he knows now.<br />They can’t concentrate: “For too long Death was our companion, he was a quick player, and each second was for the highest stakes.”<br />They feel empty, it makes them restless because they feel that people do not get it. This is a recurring theme. Even love can not help them. There is an unbridgeable gap between soldiers and non-soldiers. “We have to help ourselves.”<br /><br />They go thru life. Ernst (Birkholz, the narrator of the story) realizes when they are at a gathering of their regiment that even the fellowship of soldiers is disappearing: “They are still our comerades and at the same time they are not, that makes me sad. Everything else is destroyed in the war, but we believed in the comradeship. But now we see: what death could not bring about, life succeeds: it separates us.”<br /> <br />Remarque puts if very eloquently when he lets one of his characters fulminate against the war: “And why, ..., why?...Because we were deceived, deceived and we hardly noticed it! We were terribly misused! They said country and meant occupation plans of a greedy industry - they said honor and meant the bickering and desire for power of a handfull of overambitious diplomats and lords - they say nation and meant the action craving of unemployed generals!” <br />“With the word Patriotism they hide their empty cliche drivel, their ambition, their strive for power, their lying romanticism, their stupidity, their profiteering, and they present it as a glorious ideal!” On he goes. “The youth of the world is broken up, and in each country they believe to fight for freedom. In each country they were lied to and misused, in each country they fought for interests instead of ideals, in each country they were shot down and mutually exterminated”.<br />It is heartbreaking. It keeps coming back. Really it is depressing. Their future is dead: “Our future is dead because the youth who carried it, is dead.”<br /><br />We follow Ernst is his life to try to fit in again at home. And time and again he does not find peace. <br />As a teacher in elementary school he contemplates youth: “They came uneducated, sincere and unaware, like young animals, from their meadows, their plays and dreams in the school; still the simple law of playfulness: the most lively would be the most populat. But weeks of education would gradually force upon them another artifcial law of evaluation: the ones who, well-behaved, produced their work, would become excellent and considered the best.”<br />He is not optimistic about their future: “In the school threadmill they enjoyed a short shadow existence - to later, sink more surely into mediocracy and subaltern triviality.”<br />With women: “Ah, Love - a torch, falling into an abyss, and then shows how deep it is.”<br />So even in love he does not expect to find solace.<br /><br />His friend Ludwig: “It’s all useless, Ernst. We are broken, but the world goes on, like the war was never there. Before long and our successors in the school banks will be eager to hear stories from the war and wish they would have been there...”<br /><br />After a breakdown and illness he starts to get on the road back... In nature he finds peace and realized the beauty of it. It’s full of life. It rejuvenates him. For the first time he has experienced something like homeland and peace: “I don’t dare to believe it, took it for weakness and tiredness, but maybe with commitment, maybe we just have to be patient and be silent, and it will come to us, maybe the only thing that has not abandoned us, is really our body and the earth, and maybe all we have to do is to listen and follow it.”<br /><br />There are so many incredible descriptions of what they feel that it is too much to quote them all. The book is full of reminiscence...<br /><br />As said the journey back does end with a lighter note. There is hope. But it has cost them a lot.<br /><br />The knowledge of WWII looms over the book for me. This book was published in 1931. But it’s full of eerie foreboding of what is going to happen.<br />Upon seeing boys in the forest following an adult “leader” play acting war: “My God, we are still alive and just out, how is it possible that there <br />are already people doing this?”. It’s uncanny this foresight.<br /><br />In the end Ernst is again in nature. It’s spring. He feels inspired by it. <br />About life: “It will not bring the fulfillment of which we dreamt in our youth and what we expected after the years out there...I will be alone...I will hesitate on cross roads...stumble and fall...but I will get up and go on and not look back. Maybe I can never be happy, maybe that is crushed by the war, and I always will be a little absentminded, nowhere at home; but also I’ll not be totally unhappy, because something will always be there to sustain me, even if it’s only my hands or a tree or the breathing earth.”<br /><br />And so the book ends with an optimistic note...<br /><br /><br />The below poem (by british poet Wilfred Owen, 1893 - 1918) describes the horror of the war and the lie that it is honerable.<br />Noone told them that they would be scarred for life... (if you even survived) and never feel at home again.<br />Dulce et Decorum Est<br />Bent double, like old beggars under sacks,<br />Knock-kneed, coughing like hags, we cursed through sludge,<br />Till on the haunting flares we turned our backs<br />And towards our distant rest began to trudge.<br />Men marched asleep. Many had lost their boots<br />But limped on, blood-shod. All went lame; all blind;<br />Drunk with fatigue; deaf even to the hoots<br />Of tired, outstripped Five-Nines that dropped behind.<br /><br />Gas! Gas! Quick, boys!—An ecstasy of fumbling,<br />Fitting the clumsy helmets just in time;<br />But someone still was yelling out and stumbling<br />And flound'ring like a man in fire or lime...<br />Dim, through the misty panes and thick green light,<br />As under a green sea, I saw him drowning.<br /><br />In all my dreams, before my helpless sight,<br />He plunges at me, guttering, choking, drowning.<br /><br />If in some smothering dreams you too could pace<br />Behind the wagon that we flung him in,<br />And watch the white eyes writhing in his face,<br />His hanging face, like a devil's sick of sin;<br />If you could hear, at every jolt, the blood<br />Come gargling from the froth-corrupted lungs,<br />Obscene as cancer, bitter as the cud<br />Of vile, incurable sores on innocent tongues,—<br />My friend, you would not tell with such high zest<br />To children ardent for some desperate glory,<br />The old Lie: Dulce et decorum est<br />Pro patria mori.<br />(it is sweet and right to die for your country)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11021703832411382228" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3147169038" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3147169038" />
    <a title="Justin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7769627-justin"><img alt="Justin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372633288p2/7769627.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3147169038?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justin" href="/user/show/7769627-justin">Justin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3147169038" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10989418029188134939">Note that the version of “Der Weg züruck” that I read was the 2019 translation by Brian Murdoch entitled “The Way Back”, which, as the translator explains, more accurately reflects the soldiers’ attempts to find a way back into society.<br/><br/>This is one of the best books I have ever read, and it amazes me that I’ve read two books that match that description in less than a year, and that it has taken me decades to find them both (the other one was Larry McMurtry’s Lonesome Dove). This book, an autobiog</span>
  <span id="freeText10989418029188134939" style="display:none">Note that the version of “Der Weg züruck” that I read was the 2019 translation by Brian Murdoch entitled “The Way Back”, which, as the translator explains, more accurately reflects the soldiers’ attempts to find a way back into society.<br /><br />This is one of the best books I have ever read, and it amazes me that I’ve read two books that match that description in less than a year, and that it has taken me decades to find them both (the other one was Larry McMurtry’s Lonesome Dove). This book, an autobiographical novel, is both gripping and achingly poignant, and is thoroughly absorbing and so insightful. The writing and descriptions are superb. This is the fifth novel by Erich Maria Remarque that I’ve read; they’re all excellent (and similar in their poignancy), but this one might be my favourite now. I should stress how much I enjoyed the writing - descriptive, evocative, but incredibly straightforward and readable too.<br /><br />It starts in the final days of the First World War, vividly evoking the trenches and the daunting challenge of surviving, knowing that the war is expected to end within days. Some of the best chapters of this book are the ones that cover the end of the war and the journey home - it’s something you don’t normally get much insight into, exactly what happened when the war ended. It turns out that the Germans started withdrawing almost immediately, and within days they had marched back into Germany, the Allies (in this case healthy-looking and well-equipped Americans) marching hot on their heels (what would happen when the Americans caught up? - being in much better shape they marched faster). Once in Germany, it was a struggle then to get on trains back to home towns. Even at this stage, soldiers were still dying. This journey and these days were fascinating. Revolution was in the air. The chain of command was being replaced by soldiers’ councils. Everyone took their weapons and equipment home, and kept hold of them despite a demobilisation of sorts a few days later.<br /><br />The account of the arrival home was particularly moving, seeing family again, who were oblivious to what they had been through, leading to some difficult, heated and sometimes achingly sad encounters.<br /><br />The timeline then stretched out through the months of winter 1918/1919, and then into the summer until the book finally ends, I think, in summer 1920 - though Remarque never gives dates or even names places. The translator notes that the book seems to be set in Remarque’s childhood home of Osnabrück.<br /><br />After the initial return then, we follow Ernst, the narrator, and his comrades as they resume their school studies, become teachers, or otherwise find things to occupy their time with, amid a background of hardship, deprivation (and yet bars, cafes and nightclubs flourished amid the poverty) and uncertainty - although those who were adept at black-marketeering go on to thrive more than others. Death and violence aren’t left behind in the war at all, and some of the old soldiers (“old” despite being aged around 20) continue to die in the peace - either by suicide or in revolutionary violence, killed by former comrades who stayed in the army. Latterly there is a taste of the nationalism that would manifest in Nazism, with those too young to have been in the war romanticising and glorifying it - you can see several seeds for the next war. Throw in millions of dissatisfied veterans inured to violence and seeking purpose, excitement and comradeship, and you’ve got a recipe for the paramilitary violence of the twenties. The former soldiers in the book hanker for the lost comradeship and sense of purpose of the trenches, but they fail to reproduce it in peacetime, no matter how hard they try. People drift apart. The togetherness was perhaps an illusion.<br /><br />The soldiers’ emotional journey is one of the strongest elements of the book, as Remarque explores the effect the war has had on them, mentally and physically. These are perhaps realised most extremely when Ernst and friends visit a mental asylum, or watch a protest by wounded veterans, with very vivid descriptions. Ernst and others also regularly describe their dreams. However, most often the emotional journey is much more mundane. Ernst routinely thinks back to people and experiences, describing them in his narrative - often quite prosaic. They rail at petty officiousness by non-veterans. The characters frequently express their emptiness, their pointless existences, their struggle to find purpose, their loneliness, their quest for fulfilment, and their separation from normal society. Those are all things I could relate to from my own time after Afghanistan. They also view the war as having been pointless - a challenging conclusion for them to reach. What was it all in aid of? I also found it gave a lie to the fetishisation of commemorating the First World War which seems to have taken off in recent years - the “we will remember them” and “for our today they gave their tomorrow.” Reading this reinforces what a complex event it was, which makes it incredibly hard to adequately commemorate.<br /><br />I think I’ll finish by quoting a couple of the concluding paragraphs, which very much captures the sentiment of the book:<br /><br />“I know now that everything in life is perhaps only a kind of preparation, working in the individual, in different areas, going off into different channels, each one separate - and just as the different parts and branches of a great tree have to take the sap and move it onwards as it forces itself up, one day for us, too, there will be a rustling of the leaves and sunshine on the crown, and freedom. I want to make a start.<br /><br />“It will not be the fulfilment that we dreamt of when we were young and that we expected when we came back after the years out there. It will be a path like any other, with stony parts and good stretches, with damaged sections and with villages and fields; a pathway of work. I shall be alone. Maybe now and again I might find someone to go along some of the way with me, but probably not for all of it.<br /><br />“And it may well be that I shall often have to hoist up my pack when my shoulders are already weary; and just as often I shall find myself hesitating at a crossroads or at a border, I’ll have to leave something behind, I’ll stumble and fall; but I’ll get up again and not just lie there, I’ll go on and not turn around. Perhaps I shall never be able to be really happy again, perhaps the war knocked that out of me and I shall always be a little bit apart, not fully at home anywhere; but I think I shall never be completely unhappy, because there will always be something there to hold me up, even if it is just my own hands, or a tree, or the living earth.”<br /><br />That’s probably something we can all relate to.<br /><br />It’s achingly poignant, but a satisfyingly resolved ending, even upbeat, unlike some of Remarque’s other books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10989418029188134939" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Aram Garoghlanian was a Californian, born in Fresno on the other side of the Southern Pacific tracks. But he was also part of a large, sprawling family of immigrant Armenians--a whole tribe of eccentric uncles, brawling cousins, and gentle women. Through these unforgettable, often hilarious characters Aram comes to understand life, courage, and the power of dreams. Whether it is fierce Uncle Khosrove who yells &quot;Pay no attention to it&quot; in any situation, Uncle Melik, who tries to grow pomegranate trees in the desert, or angelic-looking Cousin Arak who gets Arma into classroom scrapes, Aram\'s visions are shaped and colored by this tum-of-the-century clan. Like Sherwood Anderson\'s Winesburg, Ohio, William Saroyan\'s brilliant short stories in My Name Is Aram work together to create a picture of a time, a place, and a boy\'s world-a truly classic       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4116.Erich_Maria_Remarque">About Erich Maria Remarque</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4116.Erich_Maria_Remarque"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1207351165p3/4116.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4116.Erich_Maria_Remarque">Erich Maria Remarque
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,854
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7817931787640582518">Erich Maria Remarque (pen name of Erich Paul Remark) is one of the best known and most widely read authors of German literature in the twentieth century. <br/><br/>Remarque's biography is essentially marked and his writing fundamentally influenced by German history of the twentieth century: Childhood and youth in imperial Osnabrück, World War I, the Weimar Republic, and most of all his exile in Switzerland </span>
  <span id="freeText7817931787640582518" style="display:none">Erich Maria Remarque (pen name of Erich Paul Remark) is one of the best known and most widely read authors of German literature in the twentieth century. <br /><br />Remarque's biography is essentially marked and his writing fundamentally influenced by German history of the twentieth century: Childhood and youth in imperial Osnabrück, World War I, the Weimar Republic, and most of all his exile in Switzerland and the United States. With the novel All Quiet On the Western Front, first published in 1929, Remarque attained world-wide recognition continuing today. <br /><br />Examples of his other novels also internationally published are: The Road Back (1931), Three Comrades (1936, 38), Arch of Triumph (1945), The Black Obelisk (1956), and Night in Lisbon (1962). <br /><br />Remarque's novels have been translated in more than fifty languages; globally the total edition comes up to several million copies. <br /><br />The complete works of Remarque are both highly interrelated with his Osnabrück background and speaking thematically of a critical examination of German history, whereby the preservation of human dignity and humanity in times of oppression, terror and war always was at the forefront of his literary creation. <br /><br />AKA:<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17900575._________________" title="Έριχ Μαρία Ρεμάρκ" rel="nofollow noopener">Έριχ Μαρία Ρεμάρκ</a> (Greek)<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8106923._________________" title="Эрих Мария Ремарк" rel="nofollow noopener">Эрих Мария Ремарк</a> (Russian)</span>
  <a data-text-id="7817931787640582518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/658974-der-weg-zur-ck">Quotes from The Road Back</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Educationalists who think they can understand the young are enthusiasts. Youth does not want to be understood; it wants only to be let alone. It preserves itself immune against the insidious bacillus of being understood. The grown-up who would approach it too importunately is as ridiculous in its eyes as if he had put on children's clothes. We may feel with our youth, but youth does not feel with us. That is its salvation.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/521504-educationalists-who-think-they-can-understand-the-young-are-enthusiasts">18 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I have been running all about; I have knocked again at all the doors of my youth and desired to enter in there; I thought, surely it must admit me again, for I am still young and have wished so much to forget; but it fled always before me like a will-o'-the-wisp; it fell away without a sound; it crumbled like tinder at my lightest touch. And I could not understand.--Surely here at least something of it must remain? I attempted it again and again, and as a result made myself merely ridiculous and wretched. But now I know. I know now that a still, silent war has ravaged this country of my memories also; I know now it would be useless for me to look farther. Time lies between like a great gulf; I cannot get back. There is nothing for it; I must go forward, march onward, anywhere; it matters nothing, for I have no goal&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/519616-i-have-been-running-all-about-i-have-knocked-again">13 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/658974-der-weg-zur-ck">More quotes&hellip;</a>
